Conditions for a Seller's Claim to a Newborn Slave-Child
Stating the ruling when seller claims child born less than six months from sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the four conditions required for a seller's claim to a child born within six months of sale to establish lineage, and distinguish between revocable and irrevocable acts by the buyer.

Core Idea
A seller's claim to a newborn slave-child is valid only if the child is alive, born within six months, not freed, and not claimed first by the buyer.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq addresses a specific scenario in slave law: when a seller claims the newborn child of a female slave he sold, and the birth occurs within six months of the sale. The Hanafi school applies a set of conditions to determine whether the seller's claim establishes lineage. Understanding these conditions illustrates how Hanafi jurists balance competing claims of ownership and lineage with the irreversibility of certain acts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the basic ruling
Read the first part: 'ولدت مبيعة لأقل من مدة الحمل... فهو ابنه، وهي أم ولده.' The ruling is that if a sold female slave gives birth within less than the minimum pregnancy period (six months) from the sale, and the seller claims the child, then the child becomes the seller's son and the mother becomes his umm walad (mother of his child, thus free at his death).
This is the default ruling when no other conditions interfere.
2
Determine the conditions for the claim
The text then lists conditions: the child must be alive, not subjected to an irrevocable act (like emancipation or tadbir), and the buyer must not have claimed the child first. Note that the child must also be born within six months (أقل من ستة أشهر).
Each condition prevents the claim from succeeding.
3
Understand the rationale for each condition
The text explains: (1) if the child is dead, lineage cannot be established because rights do not apply to the deceased; (2) if the buyer freed the child (itq or tadbir), the claim would require revoking the emancipation, which is irrevocable; (3) if the buyer claimed the child first, the child's lineage is from the buyer, and the seller cannot overturn it.
These rationales are rooted in legal principles: irrevocability of certain acts and priority of claims.
4
Distinguish revocable vs. irrevocable acts
The passage contrasts irrevocable acts (عتق, تدبير) with revocable acts (بيع, كتابة, رهن, إجارة, هبة). If the buyer performed a revocable act (e.g., sold the child), the seller's claim still establishes lineage and the revocable act is cancelled.
This distinction is crucial: only irrevocable acts block the claim.
5
Apply the rule to a scenario
Given a specific scenario, check each condition: (1) Is the child alive? (2) Was it born within six months? (3) Did the buyer perform an irrevocable act? (4) Did the buyer claim first? If all conditions are met, the seller's claim succeeds.
The claim succeeds even if the buyer denies it; the buyer's confirmation is not required.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولدت مبيعة لأقل من مدة الحمل مذ بيعت فادّعاه البائع، فهو ابنه، وهي أم ولده، أي أمة مبيعة لأقل من ستة أشهر من حين بيع. فهو ابنه بشرط أن لا يسبقه المشتري في الدعوة والحاصل أن البائع إذا ادعى ولد المبيعة، فلا يخلو إما أن تجيء به لأقل من سنتين أم لا، ثم ذلك لا يخلو إما أن يصدقه المشتري في الدعوة أم لا، وكل ذلك لا يخلو إما أن يكون الولد المدعى نسبه حياً أو ميتاً، والأول لا يخلو إما أن يوقع المشتري به ما لا يمكن نقضه كالعتق والتدبير، أو ما يمكن كالبيع والكتابة والرهن والإجارة والهبة أم لا. وكذلك الأم على هذا التقسيم إما أن تكون وقت الدعوة حية أو ميتة، فإن كانت حية، فإما أن يكون المشتري أوقع بها ما لم يمكن نقضه أو ما يمكن، فإذا ادعى البائع ولد المبيعة، ينظر إذا جاءت به لأقل من ستة أشهر، وهو حي، لم يتصف بالعتق أو التدبير، ولم يسبقه المشتري في الدعوة، ثبت النسب من البائع مطلقاً، صدقه المشتري أم لا، فالتقييد بالحياة للاحتراز عن الوفاة، حيث لا يثبت نسبه؛ لأن الحقوق لا يثبت للميت ابتداء ولا عليه، والتقييد بعدم اتصافه بالعتق أي التدبير؛ للاحتراز عما إذا كان الولد عند الدعوة عتيقاً أو مدبراً أن أعتقه المشتري أو دبره حيث لا يثبت نسبه أيضاً؛ لأن ثبوت نسبه يستلزم نقض عتقه أو تدبيره وكل منهما بعد وقوعه لا ينتقض، بخلاف ما إذا ادعى نسبه بعد أن باعه المشتري أو كاتبه أو رهنه حيث يثبت نسبه وتنتقض هذه التصرفات، والتقييد بعدم سبق المشتري البائع في الدعوة؛ للاحتراز عما إذا ادعاه قبله فإن النسب يثبت من المشتري، ولا يتصور بعده ثبوت النسب من البائع.
A sold female slave gives birth in less than the duration of pregnancy from the time of sale, and the seller claims it, then it is his son, and she is his umm walad, i.e., a sold female slave giving birth in less than six months from the time of sale. So it is his son on condition that the buyer does not precede him in the claim. The summary: when the seller claims the child of the sold female slave, it is either that she gives birth in less than two years or not. Then that is either that the buyer confirms the claim or not. And all that is either that the claimed child is alive or dead. The first (alive) is either that the buyer has done to it something irrevocable like emancipation (itq) or tadbir, or something revocable like sale, kitaba, rahn, ijarah, or hibah, or not. Similarly, the mother at the time of the claim is either alive or dead. If alive, then the buyer has either done to her something irrevocable or revocable. So when the seller claims the child of the sold female slave, it is considered: if she gives birth in less than six months, and the child is alive, not affected by emancipation or tadbir, and the buyer has not preceded him in the claim, then lineage is established from the seller absolutely, whether the buyer confirms it or not. The restriction of life is to avoid the case of death, where lineage is not established because rights are not established for the deceased initially nor against him. The restriction of not being affected by emancipation or tadbir is to avoid the case where the child at the time of the claim is freed or mudabbar, because the buyer freed him or made him mudabbar, where lineage is also not established because establishing lineage would necessitate revoking his emancipation or tadbir, and each of them after occurrence cannot be revoked. Unlike the case where he claims lineage after the buyer sold him, or made kitaba, or rahn, where lineage is established and these transactions are revoked. The restriction of the buyer not preceding the seller in the claim is to avoid the case where he claimed before him, then lineage is established from the buyer, and after that, establishment of lineage from the seller is inconceivable.
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage details the ruling when a seller claims the newborn child of a female slave he sold, provided the birth occurs within six months of the sale (the minimum period of pregnancy). The Hanafi position is that if the child is born alive, the buyer has not performed any irrevocable act (like emancipation or tadbir) on the child, and the buyer has not already claimed the child, then the seller's claim is accepted and the child's lineage is established from the seller. The seller's claim overrides the buyer's ownership because the conception is presumed to have occurred during the seller's ownership. The passage then explains each condition: if the child is dead, lineage is not established because rights cannot be established for or against a deceased person; if the buyer has freed the child, lineage is not established because that would revoke the emancipation, which is irreversible; but if the buyer sold the child, the seller's claim still establishes lineage and the sale is cancelled. If the buyer claimed the child first, the child becomes his child and the seller's claim is void.

Key Terms
العتق (al-'itq): Emancipation by the buyer that cannot be revoked; if done to the child, the seller's claim does not establish lineage.
التدبير (al-tadbir): A declaration that the slave will be free upon the master's death; similar to 'itq in being irrevocable for lineage claims.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What happens if the buyer has already freed the child before the seller claims lineage?
A) The seller's claim establishes lineage and the child remains free
B) The seller's claim is invalid and lineage is not established
C) The child becomes the seller's but freedom is revoked
D) The child remains the buyer's and lineage is established from seller

Key Takeaway
A seller's claim to a newborn slave-child establishes lineage only if the child is alive, born within six months, not freed by the buyer, and not already claimed by the buyer.

Buyer's Claim After or Simultaneous with Seller's Claim
Explaining the effect of timing on competing claims to a child.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule when the buyer claims the child simultaneously with or after the seller, and identify the condition that makes the seller's claim stronger.

Core Idea
Seller's claim based on istilad trumps buyer's present claim.

In the previous section, we saw that when a sold female slave gives birth, both seller and buyer may claim the child. This passage clarifies the effect of timing. If the buyer claims the child at the same time as the seller or after, the seller's claim prevails because it is rooted in the time of conception (istilad). But if the buyer claims first, his claim holds and the seller's is rejected.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of claims
The seller's claim (da'wa al-istilad) is based on conception during his ownership, while the buyer's claim (da'wa al-tahrir) is based on present ownership.
2
Determine the timing
If both claim simultaneously or buyer claims after, the seller's is stronger. If buyer claims first, his claim is valid and seller's is invalid.
3
Understand the reasoning
The seller's claim looks back to the time of 'uluq (conception), which is more reliable for establishing lineage. The buyer's claim is confined to the present.
4
Apply the rule
In simultaneous or subsequent buyer claim, the seller's claim is accepted and buyer's is rejected. If buyer claims first, the child's lineage is established from the buyer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن ادعاه المشتري معه، أو بعده وكذا إن ماتت الأم، البائع الولد أي مع دعوى البائع بخلاف موت الولد وعتقهما كموتهما، وإن ولدت لأكثر من ستة أشهر، المشتري الأم والولد أي الأمة المبيعة وإن ادعاه إلخ واصل بما قبله أي ولو ادعى المشتري الولد مع دعوى البائع أو بعد دعواه تكون دعوى البائع مقدماً؛ لأن دعوى البائع أسبق؛ إذ هي تستند إلى حالة العلوق؛ لكونها دعوة الاستيلاد ودعوة المشتري دعوة التحرير؛ إذ العلوق لم يكن في ملكه فتقتصر على الحال فكانت دعوة البائع أقوى، فلا تعتبر الثانية، فصار المشتري كغيره من الأجانب، فلا تصح دعواه، بخلاف ما إذا ادعى المشتري أولاً ثم ادعى البائع لا يثبت النسب من البائع؛ لاستغنائه بثبوت نسبه من المشتري، ولأن النسب لا يحتمل الإبطال بل يثبت النسب من المشتري؛ لوجود ملكه، وتثبت أميتها بإقراره، وقيل: يحتمل على أنه نكحها واستولدها ثم اشتراها. (عيني، وفتح) أو بعده: أي إذا ادعى المشتري بعد دعوى البائع، وتذكير الضمير باعتبار الادعاء. (عيني) إن ماتت الأم: أي وكذا يثبت نسب الولد من البائع إن ماتت الأم وادعاه البائع، وقد جاءت به لأقل من ستة أشهر فيأخذ البائع الولد. (عيني) بخلاف موت الولد أي بخلاف ما إذا ادعاه البائع بعد موت الولد فإنه لا يثبت نسبه منه؛ لأن الولد بعد الموت استغنى عن النسب، ولم تصر الأمة أم ولده؛ لأن الاستيلاد فرع النسب، فإذا لم يثبت النسب لم يثبت الاستيلاد والفرق أن الولد هو الأصل في هذا الباب والأم تبع له ألا ترى أنها تضاف إليه، يقال: أم الولد، ولأنها تستفيد الحرية من جهته. ألا ترى إلى قوله عليها حين ولدت مارية القبطية ولده إبراهيم فقيل له: ألا تعتقها؟ فقال: "أعتقها ولدها"، رواه ابن ماجه، فإذا كان الولد هو الأصل، كان المعتبر بقاؤه؛ لحاجته إلى ثبوت النسب، ولا يضره فوت التبع بخلاف العكس، وهو ما إذا مات الولد دون الأم، حيث لا تصح دعوته في الأم؛ لأن الحكم لا يثبت في التبع ابتداء دون متبوعه.
And if the buyer claims him with it or after it, and similarly if the mother dies – the seller (claims) the child – i.e., along with the seller's claim, unlike the death of the child, and their emancipation is like their death. And if she gives birth after more than six months – the buyer (claims) the mother and the child, i.e., the sold female slave. And if he claims etc. is connected to what precedes, i.e., even if the buyer claims the child along with the seller's claim or after his claim, the seller's claim is given precedence because the seller's claim is based on the time of conception, being a claim of istilad, while the buyer's claim is a claim of tahrir because conception was not in his ownership, so it is confined to the present. So the seller's claim is stronger, thus the second is not considered, and the buyer becomes like any other outsider, so his claim is not valid. Unlike if the buyer claimed first, then the seller claims – lineage is not established from the seller because it is already established from the buyer, and lineage cannot be invalidated; rather, lineage is established from the buyer due to his ownership, and her status as umm walad is established by his admission. It is said: it is possible that he married her and had a child from her, then bought her. (Ayni, Fath) 'Or after it': i.e., if the buyer claims after the seller's claim, and the masculine pronoun is due to considering the claim. (Ayni) 'If the mother dies': i.e., similarly, the seller's claim to the child is valid if the mother dies and the seller claims, and she gave birth in less than six months, so the seller takes the child. (Ayni) 'Unlike the death of the child' – i.e., unlike if the seller claims after the child's death, then lineage is not established from him because the child after death is independent of lineage, and the female slave does not become umm walad because istilad is subsidiary to lineage; if lineage is not established, istilad is not established. The difference is that the child is the primary in this chapter and the mother is subsidiary – do you not see that she is attributed to him? It is said: 'mother of the child'. And because she derives freedom from him. Do you not see his statement regarding Mariyah al-Qibtiyyah when she gave birth to his son Ibrahim, and it was said: 'Will you not free her?' He said: 'Her child frees her,' narrated by Ibn Majah. So since the child is primary, its survival is considered because it needs lineage, and the loss of the subsidiary does not harm it, unlike the opposite – i.e., if the child dies without the mother, then his claim in the mother is not valid because the ruling is not established in the subsidiary initially without the primary.
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage addresses several sub-rules. First, if the buyer claims the child after or simultaneously with the seller, the seller's claim prevails because it is based on conception during his ownership (da'wa al-istilad), while the buyer's claim is only a present claim (da'wa al-tahrir). If the buyer claimed first, the seller's subsequent claim is invalid because lineage is already established. Second, if the mother dies but the child is alive and born within six months, the seller's claim to the child is still valid. However, if the child dies first, the seller cannot claim lineage because the child no longer needs lineage, and the mother's status as umm walad depends on the child (she is a subsidiary). The text cites a hadith about Mariyah al-Qibtiyyah to illustrate that the child is the primary. This shows the Hanafi principle that the mother's status as umm walad follows from the child's lineage.

Key Terms
الأم ولد (umm walad): A female slave who has borne a child to her master; she becomes free upon his death.
الاستيلاد (al-istilad): The act of making a female slave an umm walad by having a child from her.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The buyer's claim is accepted because he possesses the child.
B) The seller's claim is accepted because his claim is based on istilad.
C) Both claims are rejected.
D) The child is set free.

Key Takeaway
In competing claims of lineage, the seller's claim based on conception outweighs the buyer's present claim unless the buyer claimed first.

Death of the Mother or Child: Impact on the Seller's Claim
Explaining the effect of death on the seller's claim to lineage.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the seller's claim to the child is valid when the mother dies but invalid when the child dies, and cite the hadith of Mariyah al-Qibtiyyah as evidence.

Core Idea
The child is primary; the mother's status as umm walad depends on the child's lineage.

This lesson continues the discussion of competing claims. It addresses scenarios where either the mother or the child dies. The ruling is counterintuitive: if the mother dies, the seller can still claim the child, but if the child dies, the seller cannot claim the mother. The reason is that the child is the primary (asl) and the mother is subsidiary (tabi'). The hadith of Mariyah al-Qibtiyyah supports this: the child frees the mother, not vice versa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
Scenario A: Mother dies, child alive. Scenario B: Child dies, mother alive.
2
State the ruling for each
A: Seller's claim to child is valid. B: Seller's claim to mother is invalid (lineage and umm walad status not established).
3
Analyze the reasoning
The child needs lineage for its own sake; the mother's status depends on the child. So the child's existence is crucial.
4
Cite the dalil
Hadith: 'Her child frees her' (Ibn Majah). This shows the child is the cause of the mother's freedom.
5
Apply the principle
The subsidiary cannot be established without the primary; therefore, if the child is dead, no claim on the mother is possible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن ادعاه المشتري معه، أو بعده وكذا إن ماتت الأم، البائع الولد أي مع دعوى البائع بخلاف موت الولد وعتقهما كموتهما، وإن ولدت لأكثر من ستة أشهر، المشتري الأم والولد أي الأمة المبيعة وإن ادعاه إلخ واصل بما قبله أي ولو ادعى المشتري الولد مع دعوى البائع أو بعد دعواه تكون دعوى البائع مقدماً؛ لأن دعوى البائع أسبق؛ إذ هي تستند إلى حالة العلوق؛ لكونها دعوة الاستيلاد ودعوة المشتري دعوة التحرير؛ إذ العلوق لم يكن في ملكه فتقتصر على الحال فكانت دعوة البائع أقوى، فلا تعتبر الثانية، فصار المشتري كغيره من الأجانب، فلا تصح دعواه، بخلاف ما إذا ادعى المشتري أولاً ثم ادعى البائع لا يثبت النسب من البائع؛ لاستغنائه بثبوت نسبه من المشتري، ولأن النسب لا يحتمل الإبطال بل يثبت النسب من المشتري؛ لوجود ملكه، وتثبت أميتها بإقراره، وقيل: يحتمل على أنه نكحها واستولدها ثم اشتراها. (عيني، وفتح) أو بعده: أي إذا ادعى المشتري بعد دعوى البائع، وتذكير الضمير باعتبار الادعاء. (عيني) إن ماتت الأم: أي وكذا يثبت نسب الولد من البائع إن ماتت الأم وادعاه البائع، وقد جاءت به لأقل من ستة أشهر فيأخذ البائع الولد. (عيني) بخلاف موت الولد أي بخلاف ما إذا ادعاه البائع بعد موت الولد فإنه لا يثبت نسبه منه؛ لأن الولد بعد الموت استغنى عن النسب، ولم تصر الأمة أم ولده؛ لأن الاستيلاد فرع النسب، فإذا لم يثبت النسب لم يثبت الاستيلاد والفرق أن الولد هو الأصل في هذا الباب والأم تبع له ألا ترى أنها تضاف إليه، يقال: أم الولد، ولأنها تستفيد الحرية من جهته. ألا ترى إلى قوله عليها حين ولدت مارية القبطية ولده إبراهيم فقيل له: ألا تعتقها؟ فقال: "أعتقها ولدها"، رواه ابن ماجه، فإذا كان الولد هو الأصل، كان المعتبر بقاؤه؛ لحاجته إلى ثبوت النسب، ولا يضره فوت التبع بخلاف العكس، وهو ما إذا مات الولد دون الأم، حيث لا تصح دعوته في الأم؛ لأن الحكم لا يثبت في التبع ابتداء دون متبوعه.
And if the buyer claims him with it or after it, and similarly if the mother dies – the seller (claims) the child – i.e., along with the seller's claim, unlike the death of the child, and their emancipation is like their death. And if she gives birth after more than six months – the buyer (claims) the mother and the child, i.e., the sold female slave. And if he claims etc. is connected to what precedes, i.e., even if the buyer claims the child along with the seller's claim or after his claim, the seller's claim is given precedence because the seller's claim is based on the time of conception, being a claim of istilad, while the buyer's claim is a claim of tahrir because conception was not in his ownership, so it is confined to the present. So the seller's claim is stronger, thus the second is not considered, and the buyer becomes like any other outsider, so his claim is not valid. Unlike if the buyer claimed first, then the seller claims – lineage is not established from the seller because it is already established from the buyer, and lineage cannot be invalidated; rather, lineage is established from the buyer due to his ownership, and her status as umm walad is established by his admission. It is said: it is possible that he married her and had a child from her, then bought her. (Ayni, Fath) 'Or after it': i.e., if the buyer claims after the seller's claim, and the masculine pronoun is due to considering the claim. (Ayni) 'If the mother dies': i.e., similarly, the seller's claim to the child is valid if the mother dies and the seller claims, and she gave birth in less than six months, so the seller takes the child. (Ayni) 'Unlike the death of the child' – i.e., unlike if the seller claims after the child's death, then lineage is not established from him because the child after death is independent of lineage, and the female slave does not become umm walad because istilad is subsidiary to lineage; if lineage is not established, istilad is not established. The difference is that the child is the primary in this chapter and the mother is subsidiary – do you not see that she is attributed to him? It is said: 'mother of the child'. And because she derives freedom from him. Do you not see his statement regarding Mariyah al-Qibtiyyah when she gave birth to his son Ibrahim, and it was said: 'Will you not free her?' He said: 'Her child frees her,' narrated by Ibn Majah. So since the child is primary, its survival is considered because it needs lineage, and the loss of the subsidiary does not harm it, unlike the opposite – i.e., if the child dies without the mother, then his claim in the mother is not valid because the ruling is not established in the subsidiary initially without the primary.
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage addresses several sub-rules. First, if the buyer claims the child after or simultaneously with the seller, the seller's claim prevails because it is based on conception during his ownership (da'wa al-istilad), while the buyer's claim is only a present claim (da'wa al-tahrir). If the buyer claimed first, the seller's subsequent claim is invalid because lineage is already established. Second, if the mother dies but the child is alive and born within six months, the seller's claim to the child is still valid. However, if the child dies first, the seller cannot claim lineage because the child no longer needs lineage, and the mother's status as umm walad depends on the child (she is a subsidiary). The text cites a hadith about Mariyah al-Qibtiyyah to illustrate that the child is the primary. This shows the Hanafi principle that the mother's status as umm walad follows from the child's lineage.

Key Terms
الأم ولد (umm walad): A female slave who has borne a child to her master; she becomes free upon his death.
الاستيلاد (al-istilad): The act of making a female slave an umm walad by having a child from her.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a sold slave gives birth within six months, and the child dies before any claim, can the seller still establish the slave as umm walad?
A) Yes, because the mother gave birth.
B) No, because the child's lineage is not established.
C) Yes, if the mother is alive.
D) No, because the mother is the primary.

Key Takeaway
The child's lineage is the foundation; without it, the mother's status as umm walad cannot be established.

Refund of Price and the Sahibayn's Difference
Intra-Hanafi dispute on refund of price in sale of umm walad

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the difference between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding the refund of the price in the sale of an umm walad and identify the basis (mutaqawwim/ghayr mutaqawwim) for each position.

Core Idea
The refund amount depends on whether the umm walad is considered guaranteed property — a key Hanafi disagreement.

This passage examines an intra-Hanafi dispute over the refund of the price when a seller sells his umm walad and then successfully claims the sale was invalid. The two companions differ from Abu Hanifa on whether the mother is considered guaranteed property, leading to different refund amounts. Understanding this dispute clarifies the Hanafi legal concept of mutaqawwim and its impact on void sales.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
The seller sold his umm walad (a slave mother of his child) and later claims the sale is void because she is his umm walad. The court accepts his claim. What must the seller return to the buyer?
The sale is rescinded; the question is how much of the price must be refunded.
2
State Abu Hanifa's ruling
Abu Hanifa holds that the entire price must be returned. The sale of umm walad is completely void because she is not guaranteed property (ghayr mutaqawwim) like a free woman.
For Abu Hanifa, the mother is akin to a free person, so no liability for her value.
3
Explain Abu Hanifa's reasoning
Since the mother is ghayr mutaqawwim, the contract does not transfer ownership; the buyer never becomes liable for her. The sale is void ab initio, so the buyer gets the full price back.
Abu Hanifa analogizes to sale of a free woman, which is void and requires full refund.
4
State the Sahibayn's ruling
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that the seller returns only the portion of the price corresponding to the child, not the mother's portion.
The mother remains with the buyer because her sale is valid according to them.
5
Explain the Sahibayn's reasoning
For the two companions, the umm walad is guaranteed property (mutaqawwim). Thus, the sale regarding her is valid; only the child's sale is void. The buyer therefore must return only the child's portion to avoid combining the child and its price.
They consider the mother a valid subject of sale; the buyer is liable for her.
6
Identify the root cause
The dispute stems from a difference in the legal nature of umm walad: is she mutaqawwim or ghayr mutaqawwim? Abu Hanifa sees her as non-property due to her impending freedom; the Sahibayn see her as a slave liable for compensation until freed.
This root affects not only sales but also tort liability (ghasb).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويرد المال كله عند أبي حنيفة؛ لأنه تبين أنه باع أم ولده وبيعها باطلة، ولا يضمنها المشتري؛ لأنها غير متقومة عنده كالحرة، ولهذا لا تضمن بالغصب عنده وعندهما يردّ البائع حصة الولد، ولا يرد حصة الأم؛ لأنها متقومة عندهما، فتضمن بالعقد والغصب، فتكون مضمونة عند المشتري، فإذا رد الولد دونها، يجب على البائع رد حصة ما سلم له، وهو الولد؛ لئلا يجتمع البدلان في ملكه، ولا يجب عليه حصة ما لم يسلم له، وهي الأم. (عيني، فتح)
And the entire price is returned according to Abu Hanifa, because it becomes clear that he sold his umm walad, and her sale is void, and the buyer is not liable for her because according to him she is not a guaranteed property like a free woman, and therefore she is not liable in tort (ghasb) according to him. According to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), the seller returns the portion of the child, and does not return the portion of the mother, because according to them she is guaranteed, so she is liable by contract and tort, so she is guaranteed with the buyer. So when he returns the child without her, the seller must return the portion of what is handed over to him, i.e., the child, so that two equivalents do not combine in his ownership, and he is not obliged to return the portion of what is not handed over to him, i.e., the mother.
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage explains the consequences after the seller's claim is accepted: the sale is rescinded, and the price must be returned to the buyer. However, there is an intra-Hanafi dispute about how much of the price is returned. Abu Hanifa holds that the entire price must be returned because the sale of an umm walad is void ab initio; the mother is not a guaranteed property (ghayr mutaqawwim) in his view, similar to a free woman, so the buyer cannot claim compensation for her. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) consider the mother to be guaranteed (mutaqawwim) and thus a valid subject of sale; therefore, only the child's portion of the price is returned, since the mother remains with the buyer (the sale regarding her is valid). The seller must return the portion corresponding to the child to avoid having both the child and its price simultaneously.

Key Terms
متقوم (mutaqawwim): Property that has legal value and can be subject to liability; according to Abu Hanifa, umm walad is not mutaqawwim.
غير متقوم (ghayr mutaqawwim): Not legally valuable property; akin to a free person, not compensable.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because the sale of umm walad is void and the mother is non-guaranteed
B) Because the buyer deserves compensation for the mother
C) Because the child is free
D) Because the buyer accepted the return

Key Takeaway
The refund of the price in a rescinded sale of umm walad depends on whether the mother is considered guaranteed property — a key Hanafi dispute with practical consequences.

Effect of Buyer's Emancipation on Seller's Claim of Lineage
Ruling when buyer emancipates mother or child before seller's paternity claim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which the seller's claim of paternity is valid or invalid when the buyer has emancipated the mother or child, and explain the consequence regarding umm walad status.

Core Idea
Emancipation of the child blocks the seller's claim; emancipation of the mother does not, but she never becomes the seller's umm walad.

In sales transactions involving a female slave and her child, the seller might later claim paternity of the child. The Hanafi school rules that the buyer's prior emancipation of the mother or child affects the validity of this claim. This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq clarifies the specifics and the underlying principle, even though the text is cut off at the end.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act of emancipation
Note whether the buyer freed (itq) or made tadbir (mudabbir) the mother, the child, or both. The passage treats these similarly.
Tadbir is like emancipation in effect.
2
Determine the ruling on the seller's claim
If the buyer frees only the mother, the seller's claim to paternity of the child is valid. If the buyer frees the child, the claim is invalid. If both are freed, the claim is likewise invalid because the child is the primary object.
The child is primary, the mother is subsidiary.
3
Understand the principle: lineage without istilad
Even when the seller's claim is valid, the mother does not become umm walad for the seller because the buyer's emancipation of her is irrevocable. The child's lineage can be established without the mother's status changing, as in the cases of al-maghruur (a deceived person) or a married female slave.
Istilad (status of umm walad) is not a necessary consequence of lineage.
4
Apply the analogy to tadbir
Tadbir (a contract promising future emancipation upon the owner's death) has the same legal effect as immediate emancipation for the purpose of this ruling.
5
Determine the financial consequence
If the claim is valid (i.e., buyer freed only the mother), the seller must return only the portion of the purchase price that corresponds to the child, not the mother, because the buyer's emancipation of the mother remains effective.
The text cuts off, but the principle is clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And their emancipation is like their death, so much so that if the buyer emancipates the mother without the child, and the seller claims that he is his son, his claim is valid, and his lineage is established from him, and he is judged to be free, but she does not become umm walad for the seller because the buyer's emancipation of her is not revoked, and the establishment of istilad for the mother is not a necessary consequence of the establishment of lineage for the child, because the establishment of the child's lineage can be separated from istilad, as in the case of the child of a deceived person (gharur) and the child of a married female slave. And if the buyer emancipates the child without the mother, his claim is not valid, for what we mentioned: that the child is the primary, and the mother is subsidiary. And tadbir is like emancipation. Commentary
This passage rules on situations where the buyer has performed an emancipatory act (itq or tadbir) on the mother or child. If the buyer emancipates only the mother, the seller can still claim the child, and lineage is established, but the mother does not become the seller's umm walad because the buyer's emancipation is irrevocable. The child becomes free from him. The principle is that the child's lineage can be established without requiring the mother's status as umm walad (e.g., in cases of al-maghruur or a married slave woman). However, if the buyer emancipates the child, the seller's claim is invalid because the child is the primary and once its lineage is established through buyer's emancipation, the seller cannot claim it. The passage then notes that the refund of price follows the earlier principle: since the mother remains with the buyer (her emancipation valid), the seller only returns the portion of the price corresponding to the child. The text is incomplete, but the ruling is clear.

Key Terms
المغرور (al-maghruur): A person who is deceived (gharur) in a transaction; the child's lineage can be established without the mother becoming umm walad.

Key Takeaway
The buyer's emancipation of the child bars the seller's paternity claim, while emancipation of the mother does not bar the claim for the child but prevents her from becoming the seller's umm walad.

Return of Price: Abu Hanifa vs Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute on returning full vs partial price

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi internal dispute regarding the return of price when a seller's claim that a sold slave-girl is his umm walad is accepted, and state the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
The return of price depends on whether the umm walad is considered a property (muttaqawwima) or not.

When a seller sells a slave-girl and later claims she is his umm walad, the court may accept his claim and rescind the sale. But how much of the price must the seller return? Abu Hanifa and his two disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, differed on this question, rooted in a fundamental disagreement about the legal status of the umm walad. This lesson unpacks their arguments and the final relied-upon position.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the exact ruling of Abu Hanifa regarding the amount of price to be returned when the seller's claim is accepted.
Abu Hanifa: full price is returned.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
State the position of the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) on the same issue.
Sahibayn: return only the child's portion.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Explain the basis of the Sahibayn's position: they consider the umm walad as a valid property (muttaqawwima), so the sale is valid for her share.
The mother is guaranteed by contract, so only the undelivered child's share is returned.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
Explain Abu Hanifa's argument: the umm walad is not a property (ghayr muttaqawwima), so the entire sale is void, and the buyer returns nothing; the seller returns the full price.
Like a free woman, she cannot be sold.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf and tarjih
Identify the practical difference in rulings and which position is relied upon (mufta bihi).
The author of al-Hidayah prefers full return in all cases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويرد المال كله عند أبي حنيفة؛ لأنه تبين أنه باع أم ولده وبيعها باطلة، ولا يضمنها المشتري؛ لأنها غير متقومة عنده كالحرة، ولهذا لا تضمن بالغصب عنده وعندهما يردّ البائع حصة الولد، ولا يرد حصة الأم؛ لأنها متقومة عندهما، فتضمن بالعقد والغصب، فتكون مضمونة عند المشتري، فإذا رد الولد دونها، يجب على البائع رد حصة ما سلم له، وهو الولد؛ لئلا يجتمع البدلان في ملكه، ولا يجب عليه حصة ما لم يسلم له، وهي الأم. (عيني، فتح) ورعتقهما كموتهما حتى لو أعتق المشتري الأم دون الولد فادعى البائع أنه ابنه صحت دعوته، وثبت نسبه منه، ويحكم بحريته ولا تصير أمه أم ولد للبائع؛ لأن عتق المشتري فيها لا ينتقض وليس ثبوت الاستيلاد للأم من ضروريات ثبوت النسب للولد؛ لأن ثبوت نسب الولد ينفك عن الاستيلاد كما في ولد المغرور وولد الأمة المنكوحة، ولو أعتق المشتري الولد دون الأم لا تصح دعوته؛ لما ذكرنا أن الولد هو الأصل، والأم تبع، والتدبير كالإعتاق، ثم إذا لم تبطل عتق المشتري في الأم، فالبائع يرد من الثمن ما يخص الولد خاصة، ولا يرد ما يخص الجارية بالإجماع هنا، وهذا الفرق لأبي حنيفة بين هذا وبين ما إذا ماتت الأم؛ فإن البائع فيها يرد ما يخص الولد فقط؛ لأن القاضي كذبه في الإعتاق فيما يزعم أنها أم ولده حيث جعلها معتقة للمشتري، و لم يفسخ البيع، فبقي صحيحاً، فيجب عليها ثمنها، بخلاف الموت، فإن زعم البائع فيه لم يبطل بشيء، فبقي معتبراً في حقه، فيرد جميع الثمن، وقيل: يرد جميع الثمن هنا أيضاً عند أبي حنيفة كما مر في فصل الموت، وإليه مال صاحب "الهداية". [رمز الحقائق: ۲۱۱/۲] (فتح)
The entire money is returned according to Abu Hanifa, because it becomes clear that he sold his umm walad and her sale is void, and the buyer does not guarantee her because she is not a property (muttaqawwima) according to him, like a free woman, and therefore she is not guaranteed by usurpation according to him. And according to them (Sahibayn), the seller returns the share of the child and does not return the share of the mother, because she is muttaqawwima according to them, so she is guaranteed by contract and usurpation, thus she is guaranteed with the buyer. So when the child is returned without her, the seller must return the share of what was delivered to him, i.e., the child, so that two substitutes do not combine in his ownership, and he is not obliged to return the share of what was not delivered to him, i.e., the mother. (Ayni, Fath) 'And the freeing of both of them like their death' even if the buyer frees the mother without the child, then the seller claims that he is his son, his claim is valid, his lineage is established from him, and he is judged to be free, but his mother does not become umm walad for the seller, because the buyer's freeing of her is not annulled, and the establishment of istilad for the mother is not among the necessities of establishing lineage for the child, because the establishment of the child's lineage can be separated from istilad, as in the case of the child of al-maghrur and the child of a married slave-girl. And if the buyer frees the child without the mother, his claim is not valid, because we mentioned that the child is the origin and the mother is a follower, and tadbir is like freeing. Then if the buyer's freeing of the mother is not nullified, the seller returns from the price what is specific to the child only, and does not return what is specific to the slave-girl by consensus here, and this is the distinction of Abu Hanifa between this and the case where the mother dies: in that case the seller returns only what is specific to the child, because the judge has belied him regarding the freeing in his claim that she is his umm walad, since he made her free for the buyer, and did not rescind the sale, so it remains valid, so her price is incumbent. Unlike death, for the seller's claim there was not nullified by anything, so it remains considered in his favor, so he returns the entire price. And it is said: he returns the entire price here also according to Abu Hanifa as mentioned in the section on death, and this is the inclination of the author of al-Hidayah. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 211/2] (Fath)
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the return of the price when the seller's claim of lineage is accepted. Abu Hanifa rules that the entire price is returned because the sale of an umm walad is void ab initio; the slave-girl is not a property (ghayr muttaqawwima) according to him, similar to a free woman, so the buyer does not guarantee her. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that the slave-girl is muttaqawwima, so the seller only returns the value of the child (since the child was not delivered) but keeps the value of the mother (since she was delivered to the buyer). The text then discusses scenarios where the buyer frees the mother or the child. If the buyer frees the mother and the seller later claims the child, the seller's claim is valid, but the mother does not become umm walad because the buyer's freeing is not annulled. The lineage of the child is established independently. If the buyer frees the child first, the seller's claim is invalid because the child is the origin. The text also distinguishes between the case of freeing and the case of the mother's death: in death, the seller returns the full price; in freeing, only the child's value, because the sale of the mother is upheld. The author of al-Hidayah inclines to the view that even in the freeing case, the full price is returned. This highlights the nuanced approach of Hanafi jurisprudence in balancing claims of ownership, lineage, and freedom.

Key Terms
متقومة (muttaqawwima): A property that has legal value and can be the subject of a valid sale; according to Abu Hanifa, an umm walad is not muttaqawwima.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Sahibayn, when the seller's claim of lineage is accepted, what amount of the price does the seller return?
A) The entire price.
B) Only the portion of the price attributable to the child.
C) Only the portion of the price attributable to the mother.
D) Nothing, because the sale is valid.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school has two views on returning price when a seller's claim is accepted; the relied-upon view is full return.

10 min
Seller's Claim of Lineage from a Sold Slave-Girl
Ruling on seller's claim of child's lineage with conditions and buyer's confirmation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a seller's claim of a child's lineage from a sold slave-girl, identify the conditions for its acceptance, and apply the ruling to scenarios involving timing and buyer confirmation.

Core Idea
A seller's claim of lineage is rejected unless the buyer confirms and the child is born within six months — otherwise lineage may be established but sale stands.

In the Hanafi school, a seller who claims a child born to a sold slave-girl is his own offspring faces strict conditions. The claim is not automatically accepted; it depends on when the child was born relative to the sale and whether the buyer confirms the claim. This lesson unpacks the ruling from Kanz al-Daqaiq and its commentary, distinguishing between births within six months, within two years, and beyond, and the legal consequences for the sale, the child's lineage, and the mother's status as umm walad.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule from the matn
The matn states: 'The claim of the seller is rejected unless the buyer confirms him.' This is the baseline: the seller's claim needs the buyer's confirmation to be considered.
The claim is about lineage (nisab) of the child, not just ownership.
2
Distinguish cases by time of birth
The commentary differentiates: if the child is born within six months of the sale, conception could have occurred after the sale; if born after two years, it is certain conception occurred after the sale. This affects whether the sale is annulled upon confirmation.
Six months is the minimum period of gestation in Hanafi fiqh; two years is the maximum possible gestation period according to some views.
3
Analyze the role of buyer's confirmation
The buyer's confirmation is needed to establish lineage in both timeframes, but the effect on the sale differs. Within six months, the sale is annulled (the child's lineage back to seller voids the transfer); after two years, the sale remains valid because the conception was definitely after the sale.
After confirmation, the child becomes the seller's son and is free, but the mother's umm walad status only follows if the conception was in the seller's ownership.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi difference on price return
When the sale is annulled (within six months), the price must be returned. The text mentions a difference: some narrations from Abu Hanifa require returning the entire price, while others only the portion attributed to the slave-girl. Al-Hidayah prefers the full price return in the present case.
The difference hinges on whether the seller's claim is treated as a complete repudiation of the sale or merely a partial defect.
5
Synthesize the final rulings
Summarize: (1) Child born within six months + buyer confirms → sale annulled, child is free, mother is umm walad, full price returned. (2) Child born after two years + buyer confirms → lineage established but sale valid, mother not umm walad, no price return. (3) No confirmation → claim rejected in either case.
The commentary also notes that if the child is born between six months and two years, the same rules as within six months apply unless the buyer's confirmation is absent.

Source Text

كتاب الدعوى
دعوى النسب
ردت دعوة البائع إلا أن يصدّقه المشتري، ومن ادعى نسب أحد التوأمين، ثبت نسبهما منه، فإن باع أحدهما وأعتقه المشتري، بطل عتق المشتري. صبي عند رجل، نسب التوأمين أي من المدعى المالك أي أحد التوأمين = الجارية بالإجماع هنا، وهذا الفرق لأبي حنيفة بين هذا وبين ما إذا ماتت الأم؛ فإن البائع فيها يرد ما يخص الولد فقط؛ لأن القاضي كذبه في الإعتاق فيما يزعم أنها أم ولده حيث جعلها معتقة للمشتري، و لم يفسخ البيع، فبقي صحيحاً، فيجب عليها ثمنها، بخلاف الموت، فإن زعم البائع فيه لم يبطل بشيء، فبقي معتبراً في حقه، فيرد جميع الثمن، وقيل: يرد جميع الثمن هنا أيضاً عند أبي حنيفة كما مر في فصل الموت، وإليه مال صاحب "الهداية". [رمز الحقائق: ۲۱۱/۲] (فتح) لأكثر يصدقه المشتري: [فحينئذ يثبت النسب ويبطل بيع الأمة والولد حر والأمة أم ولده. (ط)] أي إن ولدت الأمة من ستة أشهر من وقت البيع، ردت دعوة البائع؛ لاحتمال العلوق بعد البيع، فلم يوجد المصحح فيه بيقين إلا أن يصدق المشتري البائع، فحينئذ يثبت نسبه؛ لتصادقهما فيه، وتصير الأمة أم ولد له تبعاً للولد، ويبطل البيع؛ لاستناد العلوق إلى ما قبل البيع؛ لإمكانه، ولو ولدت لأكثر من سنتين من وقت البيع، لم تصح دعوة البائع؛ إذ لم يوجد اتصال العلوق بملكه يقيناً، وإن صدقه المشتري يثبت النسب؛ إذ عدم ثبوته لرعاية حقه، وإن صدقه زال ذلك المانع، ولم يبطل بيعه للجزم بأن العلوق ليس في ملكه فلا يثبت حقيقة العتق ولا حقه؛ لأنه دعوة تحرير، وغير المالك ليس من أهله، وكانت أم ولده نكاحاً. والحاصل أن رد الدعوى فيما إذا جاءت به لأكثر من ستة أشهر، و لم يصدقه المشتري، لا فرق فيه بين ما إذا جاءت به لأقل من سنتين أو لأكثر ، وإنما الفرق بينهما من جهة ثبوت الاستيلاد للأم بعد التصديق، ونقض البيع فيها، ورد الثمن عيني
Book of Claims. Claim of Lineage. The claim of the seller is rejected unless the buyer confirms him. Whoever claims the lineage of one of twins, both are established as his lineage. If he sells one of them and the buyer frees him, the buyer's freeing is invalid. A boy with a man... (commentary) ... The conclusion: rejection of the claim when she gives birth after six months and the buyer does not confirm him, there is no difference between if she gives birth before two years or after. The difference is regarding the establishment of the mother as umm walad after confirmation, and the annulment of the sale, and the return of the price in kind.
Source pages p. 43

Commentary
This is a ruling from the matn of Kanz al-Daqaiq on claims of lineage regarding a slave-girl sold. The passage first states the general rule: the seller's claim that a child born to the sold slave-girl is his own is rejected unless the buyer confirms it. The commentary then explains the reasoning: if the child is born within six months of the sale, the claim is rejected because conception could have occurred after the sale; but if the buyer confirms, the lineage is established because they agree. If the child is born after more than two years from the sale, the claim is always rejected because it is certain conception was not in the seller's ownership. If the buyer confirms in that case, lineage is established but the sale is not annulled because the conception is definitively not in the seller's ownership, so the seller cannot claim umm walad status. The commentary also mentions a difference of opinion regarding the return of the entire price versus only part.

Key Terms
دعوة البائع (da'wat al-ba'i'): The seller's claim that a child born to a slave-girl he sold is his own offspring.
استيلاد (istilad): The act of a master impregnating his slave-girl, making her an umm walad.
أم ولد (umm walad): A slave-girl who gives birth to her master's child, thereby becoming free upon his death.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The child is established as the seller's son and the sale is annulled.
B) The child is established as the seller's son but the sale remains valid.
C) The child is not established as the seller's son because the time is too long.
D) The child becomes free and the slave-girl becomes umm walad.

Key Takeaway
A seller's claim of lineage from a sold slave-girl is conditionally accepted based on buyer confirmation and the timing of birth, with annulment of sale only when birth occurs within two years.

Rulings on the Child of a Mistaken Owner (Walad al-Maghroor)
Ruling on child of mistaken owner with proof and dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for the child of a maghroor, explain the father's obligation to pay the child's value, and distinguish the Hanafi position on recovery of 'aqr from that of al-Shafi'i.

Core Idea
The child of a mistaken owner is free, but the father must pay its value to the true owner.

When a man purchases a slave woman and later discovers she was rightfully owned by another, any child born during his possession raises complex questions of ownership and compensation. The Hanafi school balances the father's good faith with the true owner's rights, producing a nuanced ruling that treats the child as free while requiring the father to pay its value. This passage also highlights a key inter-madhhab dispute regarding recovery of the 'aqr (compensation for use of the woman).

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the parties
The scenario: a man (father) buys a slave woman, she gives birth to a child, and then the woman is claimed by her rightful owner (mustahiqq). The father is the 'maghroor' (mistaken owner). Identify the three parties: father, child, and claimant.
2
State the ruling on the child's status
The child is free (hurr al-asl). This is because the father built his intercourse on a valid legal basis (ownership), so the child inherits freedom in relation to the father.
The child is considered free in origin but slave in relation to the claimant.
3
State the ruling on the father's obligation
The father must pay the child's value to the claimant. This compensates the claimant for the loss of the child as part of his property. The value is assessed on the day of litigation.
If the father dies, the value is taken from his estate. If the child dies before payment, no value is due.
4
Examine the father's recourse against the seller
The father can recover the purchase price of the slave woman and the value of the child from the seller. However, he cannot recover the 'aqr (compensation for use of the woman's body). This is the point of dispute with al-Shafi'i.
The Hanafi view: 'aqr is for pleasure taken, not a loss covered by the sale guarantee.
5
Analyze the Shafi'i disagreement
Al-Shafi'i allows the father to also recover the 'aqr from the seller, arguing it is part of the damages caused by the seller's lack of valid ownership. The Hanafi response: the 'aqr is not part of the sale; it is compensation for benefits already received, and recovering it would make the intercourse free.
The practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf): a Hanafi father cannot claim 'aqr; a Shafi'i father can.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مشتراة فاستحقت غرم الأب قيمة الولد، وهو حر، لأنه ولد مغرور؛ إذ المغرور بنى أمره على أمر صحيح في الشرع فاستوجب النظر، والأمة ملك للمستحق، والزائد متفرع عن ملكه، فاستوجب النظر أيضاً، فوجب الجمع بين حقهما بقدر الإمكان بأن يجعل الولد حر الأصل في الأب، ورقيقاً في حق المستحق، فيضمن الأب قيمته يوم الخصومة؛ لأنه يوم المنع، وتجب هذه القيمة على الأب دون الولد، حتى إذا كان الأب ميتاً يؤخذ من تركته، ولا ولاء للمستحق عليه؛ لأنه علق حر الأصل. فإن مات الولد لم يضمن الأب قيمته وإن ترك مالا وإن قتل الولد غرم الأب قيمته، وإن قتله الأب فعليه قيمته، ويرجع بالثمن وقيمته على بائعه لا بالعقر. وقيمته على بائعه أي فيما إذا ملكها بالشراء، وعلى المستأجر فيما إذا ملكها بالإجارة. وعند الشافعي يرجع بالعقر أيضا.
A purchased [slave woman] gave birth and was then claimed [by the rightful owner]; the father pays the value of the child, and he is free. Because he is a child of a mistaken owner (maghroor); since the mistaken owner built his affair on something valid in the law, he merited consideration, and the slave woman is the property of the claimant, and the increase arises from his property, so he also merited consideration. Thus it was necessary to combine both rights as much as possible by making the child free in origin regarding the father, and a slave regarding the claimant, so the father guarantees its value on the day of litigation, because it is the day of prevention. This value is owed by the father and not the child, so if the father dies, it is taken from his estate. There is no wala' for the claimant over him because he was attached as free in origin. If the child dies, the father does not guarantee its value, even if the child left wealth. If the child is killed [by another], the father guarantees its value. If the father kills him, he must pay his value. He recovers the price and its value from the seller, but not the 'aqr. Its value is upon the seller if he owned it by purchase, and upon the lessor if he owned it by lease. According to al-Shafi'i, he also recovers the 'aqr.
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This passage details the Hanafi ruling on a child born to a man who mistakenly thought he owned a slave woman (maghroor). The child is free because the union was based on a seemingly valid ownership. However, since the woman belongs to the true owner (mustahiqq), the child is considered part of his property in principle. To reconcile both parties' rights, the child is free vis-à-vis the father but slave vis-à-vis the claimant, so the father must pay the child's value. This value is assessed at the date of litigation. If the child dies, no payment is due because it was not yet taken. If the child is killed by another, the father pays value; if the father kills him, he also pays. The father can recover the price of the woman and the child's value from the seller, but not the 'aqr (compensation for use of the woman's body). Imam al-Shafi'i differs, allowing recovery of the 'aqr as well. The Hanafi argument is that the 'aqr is compensation for pleasure taken, not a defect in the sale.

Key Terms
مغرور (maghroor): A person who enters into a transaction or action based on a mistaken but legally plausible belief of ownership.
عقر ('aqr): Compensation paid for having sexual intercourse with a woman whose ownership is disputed.
مستحق (mustahiqq): The true owner who successfully claims the property (slave woman) from the purchaser.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) The child is free and the father pays its value
C) The child is free and no payment is required
D) The child is a slave belonging to the father

Key Takeaway
The child of a mistaken owner (maghroor) is free, but the father must pay its value to the true owner, and he cannot recover the 'aqr from the seller, differing from al-Shafi'i.

Liability for a Child of a Purchased Slave Woman
Rulings on the father's liability for a child born to a purchased slave woman later claimed free.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a father (purchaser) is liable for the value of a child born to a slave woman later claimed free, and explain the Hanafi position on recovering the dower (aqr) from the seller.

Core Idea
A father is liable for a child's value only when he receives a substitute or causes destruction; mere deception (ghurur) does not create a right of recourse.

This passage from Kitab al-Da'wa examines a complex scenario: a man buys a slave woman, she bears a child, then a third party proves she was free. What is the father's liability for the child? To whom can he turn for compensation? The Hanafi school articulates a nuanced set of rules based on the principle that liability follows actual prevention (man') of the true owner's rights, not mere deception. You will learn the specific conditions that trigger liability and how the Hanafi school differs from Imam al-Shafi'i on the critical issue of the dower (aqr).

Guided Path
1
Identify the core scenario and the ruling on natural death
The child dies naturally before any claim. Hanafi: father not liable even if child left property. Why? Because inheritance isn't a substitute for the child's value.
This establishes the baseline: no liability without 'prevention' (man').
2
Analyze the ruling when the child is killed
If child is killed by a third party, father is liable only if he receives blood money. If he kills the child himself, he is liable. Liability ties to receiving a substitute or causing prevention.
Receipt of blood money = substitute for the child, so liability arises proportionally.
3
Determine the father's recourse against the seller
Father recovers the price and any value he paid for children from the seller, but not the dower (aqr). The aqr is for usufructs already consumed; recovery would make intercourse free, which is invalid.
Seller is guarantor for the sold item's safety, not for usufructs.
4
Examine the Shafi'i dispute on ghurur
Shafi'i allows recovery of aqr and children's value from donor/almsgiver due to deception. Hanafi rejects using the analogy of the misleading road informant: mere deception insufficient without guarantee.
This highlights the Hanafi principle that liability requires a guarantee or substitute, not mere misrepresentation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
دعوى النسب
فإن مات الولد لم يضمن الأب قيمته وإن ترك مالا وإن قتل الولد غرم الأب ف الولد قيمته، ويرجع بالثمن، وقيمته على بائعه لا بالعقر.
أي الأب المشتري أي بثمن جارية الولد
مات الولد: في الصورة المذكورة قبل الخصومة ثم استحقت. (ط) عيني) لم يضمن الأب: لأنه لو كان مملوكاً للمستحق حقيقة، لم يكن مضموناً فمع عدم الملك حقيقة أولى أن لا يضمن. [رمز الحقائق: ٢١٣/٢]
سالماً
وإن ترك مالاً : لأن الإرث ليس ببدل عنه لا يقوم مقامه. [رمز الحقائق: ۲۱۳/۲] وإن قتل: أي لو قتل قاتل الولد، فأخذ الأب ديته، يجب عليه ضمان قيمته؛ لأن سلامة بدله كسلامة نفسه له، ومنع بدله كمتع نفسه، وإن لم يقبض شيئاً لا يضمن . شيئاً؛ لأنه لم يمنع الولد أصلاً لا حكماً ولا حقيقةً، وإن أخذ . الدية من أقل من قيمته، وجب عليه بقدره اعتباراً للبعض بالكل، وإذا قتله الأب، فعليه قيمته؛ لأن المنع قد تحقق بقتله، كما في ولد المغصوب إذا أتلفه الغاصب. (عيني، فتح، عناية) وقيمته على بائعه [ أي الذي باع الأمة بيده (معدن)] أي فيما إذا ملكها بالشراء وعلى المستأجر فيما إذا ملكها بالإجارة؛ لأن البائع صار كفيلاً بما شرط عليه من البدل؛ لأن البيع مبني على مساواة البدلين في حكم الضمان، فلما كان الثمن من جانب المشتري للبائع، وجب أن يكون المبيع سالماً للمشتري، وذلك بأن يجعل البائع كفيلاً بسبب تملك البدل، فصار كأنه قال للمشتري: إن الحكم قد ثبت لك، فإن ضمنك أحد بدعوى باطلة، فأنا ضامن لك بما ضمنك، وكذا إن هلكت عند المشتري، فضمنه المستحق قيمتها وقيمة الأولاد، يرجع على البائع بالثمن، وبما ضمنه من قيمة الأولاد، ولا يرجع عليه بقيمة الجارية؛ لأن أخذ قيمتها منه كأخذ عينها، وفيه لا يرجع على البائع إلا بالثمن فكذا هذا، وكذا إذا زوجها رجل على أنها حرة، ثم استحقت، يرجع الأب على المزوّج بقيمة الولد؛ إذ الاستيلاد مبني على التزويج وشرط الحرية، فكان الشارط صاحب علة فنزل كالقائل: ما هذا العقد فعلي، بخلاف مجرد الإخبار بالحرية من الزوج أو منها، حيث يكون الولد رقيقاً، ولا يرجع على المخبر بشيء؛ لعدم الغرور ؛ لأنه يكون بأحد أمرين بالشرط أو المعاوضة. (عيني، فتح) لا بالعقر: أي لا يرجع المشتري على البائع بالعقر الذي أخذه منه المستحق؛ لأنه لزمه باستيفاء منافعها وهي لیست من أجزاء البيع، فلم يكن البائع ضامنا لسلامته، وعند الشافعي: يرجع؛ لأنه ضمان لزمه بفوات السلامة، قلنا: العقر عوض عما استوفى من منافع البضع فلما . رجع به سلم له المستوفى مجاناً، والوطء في ملك الغير لا يجوز يسلم له مجاناً، ولا يرجع على الواهب والمتصدق إذا ملكها بالهبة والصدقة بشيء من قيمة الأولاد وعند الشافعي يرجع. رمز الحقائق: ٢١٤/٢] لأن الغرور قد تحقق من الواهب أو المتصدق بإيجابه الملك له فيها وإخباره أنها مملوكة، قلنا: مجرد الغرور لا يكفي للرجوع؛ لأن من أخبر إنساناً أن هذا الطريق آمن فسلكه فأخذ اللصوص ماله لم يرجع على المخبر بشيء. (فتح)
Chapter of Claim of Lineage. If the child dies, the father does not guarantee his value even if the child left property. And if the child is killed, the father pays his value, and he recovers the price and the child's value from the seller, not the dower (aqr). (That is the father who is the purchaser, i.e., from the price of the slave woman of the child.) (The child dies: in the mentioned scenario before litigation then the mother is claimed.) (He does not guarantee the father: because if the child were truly owned by the claimant, it would not be guaranteed, so without true ownership it is even more so not guaranteed.) (And if he left property: because inheritance is not a substitute for it, it does not stand in its place.) (And if he is killed: if the killer of the child is killed and the father takes the blood money, he must guarantee its value; because the safety of its substitute is like the safety of itself, and prevention of its substitute is like prevention of itself. If he does not receive anything, he does not guarantee anything; because he did not prevent the child at all, neither in judgment nor in reality. If he takes the blood money less than its value, he owes proportionally, considering the part as the whole. If the father kills him, he owes his value because prevention occurred by his killing, as in the case of a child of a usurped slave woman if the usurper destroys it.) (And its value is upon the seller: that is, if he owned her by purchase, and upon the lessee if he owned her by ijarah, because the seller becomes a guarantor for what is stipulated from the substitute; because sale is based on equality of substitutes in the ruling of guarantee. So when the price is from the buyer to the seller, the sold item must be safe for the buyer, by making the seller a guarantor due to receiving the substitute, as if he said to the buyer: 'The ruling is established for you; if someone makes you liable due to a false claim, I am your guarantor for what you were made liable for.' Similarly, if the child perishes with the buyer and the claimant makes him liable for its value and the value of children, he recovers from the seller the price and what he was made liable for of the value of the children, but does not recover the value of the slave woman; because taking its value from him is like taking the 'ayn (the slave woman herself), and in that case he only recovers the price from the seller, so likewise here. Also, if a man marries her as a free woman, then she is claimed, the father recovers from the husband the value of the child; because giving birth is based on marriage and the condition of freedom, so the condition maker is the owner of the cause, as if the condition says: 'This contract is my responsibility.' This is unlike mere informing by the husband or her that she is free, where the child remains a slave and he does not recover anything from the informer due to absence of deception (ghurur); because it is either by condition or exchange.) (Not the dower: meaning the purchaser does not recover from the seller the dower that the claimant took from him; because it was due for enjoyment of her usufructs, which are not part of the sale, so the seller was not a guarantor for its safety. According to al-Shafi'i: he recovers because it is a guarantee incurred due to loss of safety. We say: the dower is compensation for the usufructs of the vulva; if he recovers it, the usufruct becomes free, and intercourse in the property of another is not allowed to be free. And he does not recover from the donor or the alms-giver if he owned her by gift or charity anything of the value of the children; according to al-Shafi'i he recovers. Because deception occurred from the donor or alms-giver by granting him ownership and informing him she is owned. We say: mere deception is insufficient for recourse; because if someone tells a person that this road is safe, and he travels it and thieves take his money, he does not recover anything from the informer.)
Source pages p. 46

Commentary
This passage from Kitab al-Da'wa discusses rulings related to claims of lineage concerning a child born to a purchased slave woman who is later claimed by a third party. The scenario: a man buys a slave woman (jariya), she gives birth to a child, then a third party proves that the woman was free (istihqaq). The father (purchaser) is liable for the child's value only if he prevented the full right of the true owner. If the child dies naturally before any claim, the father is not liable even if the child left property, because inheritance is not a substitute for the child. If the child is killed by a third party, the father must pay its value if he receives blood money from the killer; if he does not receive anything, he is not liable. If the father himself kills the child, he must pay its value. The father can recover from the seller the price and any value he paid for children, but not the dower (aqr) he might have paid for the mother's usufructs, because the aqr is compensation for enjoyment, not part of the sale. A dispute with Imam al-Shafi'i is mentioned: Shafi'i holds that the father can recover the aqr and the value of children from a donor or alms-giver due to deception (ghurur), but the Hanafi position is that mere deception does not entitle recourse, citing an analogy to a person who misleads about a road being safe. The passage emphasizes the principle that the seller is a guarantor for the safety of the sold item, and the father's liability is tied to actual prevention of the child from the true owner.

Key Terms
عقر (aql): Dower or compensation paid for the usufructs of a slave woman's vulva, which is her value for sexual enjoyment.
غرور (ghurur): Deception or misrepresentation that induces a person to act to his detriment.
قيمة (qimah): Monetary value; here specifically the market value of the child at the time of the relevant event.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When a purchased slave woman is later claimed as free and her child dies naturally before any claim, what is the Hanafi ruling regarding the father's liability for the child's value?
A) He is liable for the full value.
B) He is liable only if the child left property.
C) He is not liable at all.
D) He is liable for half the value.

Key Takeaway
A father's liability for a child's value is contingent on actual receipt of a substitute or direct causation; the dower (aqr) is non-recoverable as it compensates consumed usufructs.

Ruling on 'Kadhā Kadhā' (Two Ambiguous Numbers Without Conjunction)
Explains ruling for repeating 'kadhā' without conjunction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for two 'kadhā' without conjunction and explain the reasoning based on kināyah and minimum number principle.

Core Idea
Two 'kadhā' without conjunction equal eleven dirhams—the minimum compound from two ambiguous numbers.

This lesson examines the legal effect of using the ambiguous term 'kadhā' twice without the conjunction 'wa' in an acknowledgment of debt. The Hanafi school applies a principle of taking the minimum possible number to avoid imposing an unproven burden. We will see how this leads to eleven dirhams, and how other schools differ.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The act is acknowledging a debt with the ambiguous phrase 'kadhā kadhā dirham'. The ruling is that eleven dirhams are immediately obligatory (wajib) according to the Ẓāhir al-Riwāyah.
Other Hanafi positions exist (twenty or one hundred) but the muftā bihī is eleven.
2
Analyze the linguistic nature of 'kadhā'
'Kadhā' is a kināyah (indirect expression) for an unknown number. Its minimum implication is two, not one.
The Shāfiʿī school treats 'kadhā' as referring to a single unit, leading to one dirham.
3
Apply the Hanafi principle of minimum interpretation
When two ambiguous numbers are mentioned without conjunction, the minimum compound number is taken. The smallest possible total from two numbers each at least two is 2+9=11 (since compound numbers from 11 to 19 are possible).
This follows the maxim that ambiguity is resolved by the minimum to avoid imposing unproven obligation.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi and cross-madhhab ikhtilaf
Some Hanafis (al-Ikhtiyār) say twenty by qiyās; a narration from Muhammad says one hundred if 'dirham' is in khafḍ. Shāfiʿīs and Ḥanbalīs hold one dirham (or a fraction in khafḍ).
The relied-upon position is eleven, supported by the majority of mashāyikh.

Source Text

In al-Ikhtiyār: twenty is incumbent upon him, and it is the qiyās, because 'kadhā' is used for number customarily, and the smallest uncompounded number mentioned after it with nasb is twenty, and if he mentions it with khafḍ, it is narrated from Muhammad that one hundred is incumbent upon him. (ʿAynī)] because 'kadhā' is a kināyah for an unknown number, and when he repeated it, he has acknowledged two unknown numbers with no conjunction between them, and the minimum of two numbers like that from the interpreted [numbers] is eleven, and their maximum is nineteen, so the minimum is incumbent upon him without clarification, and the increase depends on his clarification. According to al-Shāfiʿī, one dirham is incumbent upon him.

Commentary
This passage presents the ruling for when someone says 'kadhā kadhā dirham' (so many, so many dirhams) without the conjunction 'wa'. The phrase 'kadhā' is a kināyah (indirect expression) for an unknown number. When repeated without conjunction, it represents two separate unknown numbers. The minimum compound number that can be formed from two unknown numbers without conjunction is eleven (e.g., 'kadhā' could be 5 and 6, but the smallest possible total is 11 because each 'kadhā' must be at least 2). The relied-upon Hanafi position, as explicitly stated in the commentary from Fatḥ and ʿInāyah, is that eleven dirhams are obligatory. Some Hanafī scholars, as mentioned in al-Ikhtiyār, opined twenty based on qiyās, but the established position is eleven. If the word 'dirham' is in the accusative case (nasb), the default is twenty after 'kadhā'? Actually the text says 'the smallest uncompounded number mentioned after it with nasb is twenty', but in the context of 'kadhā kadhā', the default is eleven. There is a narration from Imam Muhammad that if the word is in the genitive (khafḍ), one hundred dirhams are obligatory. The Shāfiʿī and Ḥanbalī schools (according to Imam al-Shāfiʿī and Imam Aḥmad) hold that only one dirham is due when the word is in the nominative or accusative, and a fraction (part of a dirham) when in the genitive. The passage emphasizes that the Hanafī ruling is based on the principle of taking the minimum possible number from the ambiguous expression to avoid imposing a burden without certainty, following the maxim that ambiguity is resolved by the minimum unless clarified.

Key Terms
كذا (kadhā): A kināyah (indirect expression) for an unknown number; here it is used to indicate an unspecified numerical amount.

Key Takeaway
Two instances of 'kadhā' without 'wa' create a debt of eleven dirhams by default under the Hanafi school, based on the minimum interpretation of kināyah.

Ruling on 'Kadhā wa Kadhā' (With Conjunction)
Explains ruling for repeating 'kadhā' with 'wa' conjunction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for the ambiguous expression 'kadhā wa kadhā' and explain its reasoning based on the conjunction separating two unknown numbers.

Core Idea
When someone says 'kadhā wa kadhā', the conjunction requires a minimum of twenty-one dirhams.

The expression 'kadhā wa kadhā' is a kināyah (indirect expression) for an unknown number. When used in a debt acknowledgment, its interpretation differs among schools. This lesson explains the Hanafi position that the minimum is twenty-one dirhams and contrasts it with the Shafi'i view.

Guided Path
1
Identify the expression and its structure
Recognize that 'kadhā wa kadhā' consists of two ambiguous numbers (kadhā) joined by the conjunction 'wa'. The 'wa' indicates two separate numbers.
Without the conjunction, it would be 'kadhā kadhā' with a different ruling.
2
Determine the minimum value of each 'kadhā'
In Hanafi usul, the minimum value of an ambiguous 'kadhā' is ten. The conjunction implies two numbers, so the minimum compound is 10 + 11 = 21. (The second number is one more than the first in the minimal interpretation.)
The maximum is not specified in the phrase; it defaults to ninety-nine without clarification.
3
Apply the ruling to the debt acknowledgment
If someone says 'for so-and-so upon me is kadhā wa kadhā dirham', twenty-one dirhams are immediately obligatory. Any additional amount requires clarification from the debtor.
The debtor may later specify a higher number, but the minimum is due unless proven otherwise.
4
Contrast the Shafi'i position
The Shafi'i school holds that 'kadhā wa kadhā' obligates only two dirhams, interpreting 'kadhā' as one dirham and the conjunction as simple repetition. This is a legitimate ikhtilaf between schools.
The practical consequence is a different amount due in court cases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كذا وكذا أحد وعشرون، وكذا إلخ [ أي لو قال: لفلان علي كذا وكذا والواو واحدة لزمه أحد وعشرون. (ط، عيني)] لأنه فصل بين العددين المجهولين بحرف العطف، وأقل ذلك من العدد المفسر أحد وعشرون وأكثره تسعة وتسعون، فالأقل يلزمه من غير بيان، والزيادة تقف على بيانه، وعند الشافعي: يلزمه در همان [ رمز الحقائق: ٢١٧/٢] (فتح)
Kadhā wa kadhā is twenty-one. 'Kadhā wa kadhā' etc.: [i.e., if he said: 'For so-and-so upon me is kadhā wa kadhā and the wāw is one,' twenty-one is incumbent upon him. (Ṭ, ʿAynī)] because he separated the two unknown numbers with the conjunction, and the minimum of that from the interpreted number is twenty-one, and its maximum is ninety-nine, so the minimum is incumbent upon him without clarification, and the increase depends on his clarification. According to al-Shāfiʿī: two dirhams are incumbent upon him. [Rāz al-Ḥaqāʾiq: 217/2] (Fatḥ)
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
This passage gives the ruling when someone says 'kadhā wa kadhā' (so many and so many) with the conjunction 'wa'. The presence of 'wa' indicates that two separate numbers are being mentioned, but each is still ambiguous. The minimum compound number that can be formed from two unknown numbers with conjunction is twenty-one (e.g., 10 and 11). According to the Hanafī school, twenty-one is immediately obligatory, and any additional amount requires clarification from the speaker. The Shāfiʿī school holds a different view: the phrase 'kadhā wa kadhā' obligates only two dirhams, because they interpret 'kadhā' as referring to a single dirham and the repetition indicates two dirhams. This ruling is found in the commentaries Fatḥ al-Qadīr and al-ʿInāyah on Kanz al-Daqāʾiq.

Key Terms
كذا وكذا (kadhā wa kadhā): Two ambiguous numbers with the conjunction 'wa', used as a kināyah for an unknown pair of numbers.

B) Twenty-one dirhams
C) Two dirhams
D) One hundred dirhams

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, the ambiguous phrase 'kadhā wa kadhā' obligates a minimum of twenty-one dirhams due to the conjunction splitting the expression into two numbers each minimally ten.
